C. Priorities for Programs and Services
Coveraae of Basic Services
3.11     A major thrust of the reform program and expenditure plan is the
expansion of peripheral units to increase the proportion of the population
within 8 kms of a health facility, plus an increase in the number of
peripheral staff. New peripheral facilities will be added only in areas
where currently none exist; existing dilapidated health centers, maternity
homes and rural dispensaries currently under the administration of local
authorities will be upgraded. Recognizing that housing is a major
constraint to the posting of staff to remote areas, the Government's
strategy also includes provision for construction of some staff housing
(para 2.42). As a result of financial constraints and in line with its
emphasis on peripheral services, the MOH is reconsidering its targets for
the planned expansion of curative institutions and will restrict the
expansion of hospitals to the two districts where the need is greatest.
Proaram Strengthening
3.12     Primary Health Care.  The priorities for the PHC program are
closely connected to the planned extension of basic service coverage to all
24 districts of the country. The number of HSAs will be increased
substantially so as to reach a ratio of 1 HSA to 250 households by the year
2000. The revised plan for the period up to 1995/96 includes provision for
a total of 3,500 HSAs, of which about two-thirds will be women. The HSAs
will provide a somewhat broader range of community health services than at
present, but they will continue primarily to be health communicators and



- 30 -
enablers rather than service providers. They will also continue to work
closely with the CHVs. The MOH is presently developing the detailed
strategy for the phased implementation of this program, covering the
training, placement, support and supervision of the HSAs (para 2.41, 2.42)^
This program will have a significant impact on the health status of
communities.
3.13      To improve the supervisory and logistical support for the
community-based workers, there will be (a) continuation of outreach
activities from the nearest health facility, based on the principle of a
fixed day per week/fortnight; (b) continuing education and supportive
supervision of the HSAs and peripheral field workers through fixed,
prearranged schedules; (c) regular interaction of all peripheral health
workers, including the HSAs, with the district management team on a fixed
date each month; and (d) a continuous flow of IEC materials to reinforce
their training and to provide for dissemination in the field.
3.14      Maternal and Child Health.  The Government's strategy for MCH is
to build on activities already underway, but also to move further to
community-based programs to improve the health status particularly of rural
women and children. The increase in the number of community-based workers
will improve access to MCH services. The strengthening of a variety of
media and communication campaigns, together with training activiiies, will
raise the consciousness of women about the importance of post- as well as
ante-natal care, and of good nutritional and child care practices (paras
2.44, 4.08). By placing the availability of MCH services at closer range,
the Government hopes to ensure greater utilization of them and to
strengthen maternity referral services. The strategy is to focus attention
on women's health needs through communication campaigns and training
efforts, and to raise women's consciousness about child timing and spacing,
nutrition and energy conservation, and ways to promote safe motherhood.
3.15      Malaria.  Both to strengthen the ongoing USAID supported program
and also to plan for the future, the MOH will introduce various changes in
its malaria program. These will include: (a) extension of the availability
of chloroquine to the community level through the use of HSAs; (b)
replacement of chloroquine as the primary drug for under-five children by
other drugs, wherever there are high rates of chloroquine resistance; (c)
redeployment of the drug resistance monitoring teams to update the
Ministry's knowledge about the current status of the disease and
particularly the prevalence of resistant strains; and (d) development of a
system for the continuous monitoring of drug resistance and the prevalence
of both symptomatic and asymptomatic malaria (para 2.45). This program is
already receiving some financial support from USAID and WHO.
3.16      AIDS.  To overcome the fragmentation of related programs, the
Government's strategy in the AIDS control program is to make a concerted
effort to integrate it with the activities of other relevant departments of
the MOH and other ministries (para 2.46). The new progr&m which is
supported by USAID and other bilateral donors will focus on trying to halt
the spread of HIV infection due to sexual contact, the use of contaminated
blood and perinatal transmission, and to expand education activities and
home-based care for the sick.



- 31 -
3.17      Child Spacing.  The Government's overall goal was to expand and
strengthen CS services in order to achieve a contraceptive prevalence rate
of 10 percent by 1992. In designing this project, however, it was felt
that it would be more realistic to aim to achieve this target by 1995\96,
with an interim target of six percent by 1992. To that end, the Government
plans to increase the awareness, access, acceptance and use of CS services
through widening the range of contraceptive methods, strengthening the
management of CS services, expanding service delivery points, extending the
pilot community-based contraceptive distribution program to all districts,
and increasing awareness of child spacing through the newly created Family
Welfare Council. The training curricula of all health workers will be
revised as necessary to ensure that CS is included. Also, in order to
monitor progress towards its contraceptive target, the Government will
undertake another family formation survey (para 2.47 et seq. and para
4.08).
3.18      Women In Development.  Recognizing the important role of women
in Malawi, and to address the issues raised in para 2.50-2.51, the
Government's strategy for WID are: (a) within the health sector, to
strengthen the MCH program and particularly to increase substantially the
number of HSAs, ENMs and CHNs in the rural areas to promote more woman-to-
woman contact; (b) outside of the health sector, to increase female access
to education especially at the secondary level, and (c) improve the quality
of the work carried out by the MOCS. The WID program of the MOCS already
has an important extension network, and it could have a substantial impact
on the lives of rural women. To do this, however, it will be necessary to
strengthen considerably the WID division's institutional capacity to
develop programs to increase women's access to economic opportunities,
credit and appropriate technologies. The strategy also requires that the
training capacity of the Magomero Community Development College be upgraded
to focus on practical training and the development and demonstration of
appropriate technology. In addition, demonstration programs will be
supported to assess, and increase awareness of, the economic opportunities
available to womenx (para 2.51 and 4.09).
3.19      Nutrition.  Recognizing the critical importance of addressing
malnutrition problems at the community level (paras 2.52-2.53), the
Government plans to implement a nation-wide program of community-based
growth monitoring and nutrition education by 1995. This program will build
on the ongoing experience of the 1,500 CHVs already trained and deployed,
and will involve the training of an additional 5,500 CHVs. To complement
this activity and to address the underlying problem of severe household
food insecurity, the Government's nutrition strategy also includes support
for three models of additional inputs to be provided and evaluated in areas
where CHVs are operating. A national nutrition strategy will be drafted by
1995, based on a continuous evaluation of the work of the CHVs and of the
three food security intervention models. The models are as follows:
(a) An ongoing community-level food supplementation program for the
provision of food packages to at-risk children and mothers will be
extended in certain areas (para 4.10).



- 32 -
(b) The MOCS will undertake a program in selected villages of three dis-
tricts to expand income generation opportunities for women through a
revolving credit scheme, and to reduce the caloric expenditure of
these women through bio-gas production (to reduce the need for wood
hauling) and the provision of hand-operated maize grinders (to reduce
the need for hand-pounding) (para 4.10).
(c) The ongoing pilot area-based integrated programs will be expanded by
the MOLG. This will involve the provision of a package of hybrid
maize and soybean seed, fertilizer and food to particularly food-
insecure households (para 4.10).
3.20      Iodine deficiency disorders are most efficiently addressed
through salt iodination. As indicated in para 2.54, iodination is
presently inhibited by the high retail price of salt in rural areas. The
Government's medium-term strategy is to carry out a systematic study to
identify means of reducing these prices, implement necessary measures and,
when prices have been substantially reduced, initiate salt iodination. As
an interim strategy to deal with IDDs and other micro nutrient deficiencies
the MOH has developed a five-year project for micronutrient supplementation
(para 4.10).
Manpower Development
3.21      The shortage of health manpower continues to represent one of
the major constraints to the development of health services in Malawi (para
2.55). Of particular concern to the MOH is the limited supply of those
cadres essential to the delivery of front-line health services, namely:
medical assistants, ENMs and health assistants. An immediate priority for
the Government is to increase the supply of these cadres through an
expansion of basic training capacity and through an improved utilization of
the existing health training institutions. By 1993/94, the expanded
training capacity will result in incremental annual intakes of 215 medical
assistants, 150 additional ENMs, 15 health assistants, and 65 registered
nurses, in addition to small increased numbers of trainee technicians and
technical assistants for the pharmacy, dental and laboratory services.
However, the impact of the increased training throughput will only begin to
be felt during the mid-1990's, due both to construction time and to the
two-four year durations of training. By 1996, based on the proposed
expansion of training capacity, the supply of manpower within the
categories listed above will still only have reached less than 60 percent
of the total projected requirements (Annex Tll) (para 4.11). The
Government's strategy recognizes that the phasing of its development
program must take into account these continued manpower constraints. In
deploying the additional manpower, the MOH will give priority to the
provision of staff for existing health centers which remain inoperative due
to lack of manpower, and to bringing the staffing levels of clinics
currently managed by local authorities up to the minimum standards
operative in MOH facilities. Together with the need to staff facility
developments already underway, these priorities will fully absorb the
increases in manpower supply over the next few years. By 1995/96, however,
the supply of manpower will be sufficient to staff some limited, priority
development of hospital services.



- 33 -
Pharmaceuticals
3.22      The Government's national pharmaceutical program for the period
up to 1995 provides a sound basis for addressing its pharmaceutical
problems (see para 2.58). Data from the drug utilization study will be
used to improve the rational use of drugs, and the new computerized stock,
inventory control and procurement system will improve efficiency. In order
to decentralize the pharmaceutical supply system, however, there is also
need for additional training activities and for some improvements to be
made to the existing CMS buildings in Lilongwe and Blantyre; these will be
carried out during the next two years. A quality control mechanism for all
imported drugs will also be put in place. Additionally, there will be a
review of the production unit in Lilongwe and a rationalization of the
product range in Blantyre.   The Government is also taking steps to reduce
its debt to the CMS and to improve its financial management so that it can
once again become self-financing. The result of these measures would be
major improvements in the efficiency and cost-effectiveness of the
pharmaceutical system. During the next five years, the decentralized
distribution system will become operational, the required manpower for the
pharmaceutical sector would be trained, and a quality control laboratory
and a more efficient local production unit would become operational (para
4.14). As a first step in obtaining additional external support for the
national pharmaceutical program, a request has been submitted to the
Government of the Netherlands for funding activities to rationalize drug
use and thereby optimize resource utilization, short-term consultants to
prepare a plan of operations for the implementation of the decentralized
distribution system, and the provision of a new computerized inventory
control system for the central and regional stores.
Information, Education and Communications
3.23      The crucial role of IEC has already been recognized by the
Government, and the strategy of the MOH is now to emphasize both
interpersonal communication and the use of the mass media in the promotion
of health, disease control and CS (paras 2.59-2.60). This strategy is an
essential part of the PHC approach adopted by the Government, and it is
based on the existing institutional system. In order to create an overall
framework for the entire health education effort, the Government has
already started to develop a coordinated communication plan with clear
priorities and a detailed implementation timetable. The strategy would be
to assist the MOH to decentralize HEU/IEC activities to the community level
by using a two-pronged approach aimed at targeting IEC meseages and
materials towards: (i) clients attending health facilities and (ii) women
and children in villages. DHEOs and HSAs, along with other key community
level workers, would be used to provide strong interpersonal communication
outreach at the local level. This approach requires the strengthening of
the role of all health workers as educators, through the use of clinic and
community oriented IEC activities as set out in annual and monthly work
programs (para 3.13). In addition, the MOH plans to build up the
competence of the HEU to increase its capacity to formulate information and
education programs, as well as to carry out motivational training
activities for all health workers. The HEU would thus be strengthened to
operate as the principal agency responsible for all IEC programs.   The



- 34 -
Government's IEC plan also includes measures to widen the access and
exposure of critical target groups to audio-visual materials, and to
improve coordination mechanisms between the various responsible Government
ministries involved in media work (para 4.13).
Management and Decentralization
3.24      The MOH has started to address its internal management problems
in a variety of ways, and external assistance is already being provided by
USAID to support the Ministry's efforts to strengthen capabilities in the
planning and development of health manpower. Also, to strengthen the
coordination of manpower planning, training, personnel administration and
the in-service training activities of the different PHC programs (paras
2.61-2.64), the MOH has decided to establish a Manpower Planning and
Development unit within its administrative branch (para 6.01(f)). This
unit will have responsibility for developing, periodically reviewing and
updating long-term projections of staffing requirements, phased according
to the development program and affordable within estimates of future
financial resources. From these staffing targets, long-term training
strategies and plans will be developed. The MOH will also develop a
manpower information system to support manpower planning and personnel
management decisions at all levels (para 4.12). This system will
complement a system presently being developed by the DPMT, but will also
link manpower, service and financial data for each health facility and each
management level. In addition, it will include PHAM as well as Government
health staff. The design of the MOH system will take into account the
plans for the decentralization of personnel functions and will be used at
district, regional and central levels of the MOH.
3.25      To strengthen personnel management, a review of personnel
functions will be undertaken during 1991 to determine those
responsibilities that should be decentralized from the headquarters to the
regional and district health offices. Based on these recommendations, an
Implementation strategy and plan will be developed in coordination with
similar plans for the decentralization of planning and financial
responsibilities, and covering the future roles and responsibilities of
each level, together with staffing, equipment and training needs. A
personnel procedures manual will be developed as a guide for health
managers and personnel officers at each level.
3.26      The Government's policy is to decentralize all health services
below hospitals to the district level and to reduce the inefficiencies
associated both with the current parallel systems of the MOH and the local
authorities, and with the lack of coordination within the MOH. Although
the Government plans eventually for the local authorities to play a greater
role in the management of health service delivery, it also recognizes that
they need first to strengthen the financial and human resource base of the
local authorities (paras 2.63, 4.12). Therefore, recognizing that the
local authorities do not have the expertise to manage health activities,
the Government intends: (a) to give responsibility for all MOH and local
authority health services below the hospital level to be managed and
administered by strengthened district management teams under the DHOs (the
teams would, therefore, each have responsibility for the administration,



- 35 -
management and technical supervision of all health peripheral workers
within their respective district); (b) to provide the MOLG with the
resources to meet directly the salary of health workers of Local
Authorities; and (c) to provide a line item within the MOH budget to meet
the non-salary recurrent cost of running the Local Authorities' health
facilities.
Resource Mobilization and Efficiency
3.27      The congestion at the main hospitals largely stems from lack of
accessible and/or acceptable periphe.al facilities providing good quality
care from well trained staff with adequate drugs and diagnostic facilities.
To deal with this problem, the Government has now decided in the rural
areas to decentralize health services and increase the quality of outreach
work and the number of peripheral facilities. At the district level, the
Government will also rationalize the referral procedures between MOH and
PHAM operated facilities (para 2.64). In the urban areas served by the
central hospitals, the Government will set up satellite health centers
properly staffed and with adequate drug and diagnostic facilities.
Investments will also be made to increase the operational efficiency of the
major hospitals. In addition, to encourage patients to seek care at
appropriate levels, the Government intends to introduce some cost sharing
measures, once the satellite system is in place (para 2.66). The
decongestion of the central hospitals will lead to considerable
improvements in the efficiency of the entire health system, by permitting
the higher level facilities to deal with the patients most in need (paras
2.66 and 4.15). The Government intends to increase the fees for private
patients annually so as to keep pace with inflation, as well as selectively
introduce charges for drugs.



- 36 -
IV. THE PROPOSED PROJECT
A. Obiectives, Rationale, and Summary Description
4.01      The proposed project would support the Government's efforts to
implement the public expenditure and policy reform programs for the sector,
as outlined in Chapter III, and thereby address the issues discussed in
Chapter II. Specifically, the project's objectives are to improve the
juality, access, efficiency and effectiveness of the sector through: (a)
strengthening of basic programs, focussing on PHC, MHC, malaria control,
the AIDS program, CS, nutrition interventions, and WID activities; (b)
strengthening of support services, focussing on manpower development
issues, pharmaceuticals and IEC; (c) efficiency improvements, focussing on
management strengthening, hospital decongestion, cost sharing and budgetary
reforms; and (d) support to the Government's social dimensions of
adjustment initiative.
4.02      The project would assist the Government to proceed with the
second phase of its current ten-year (1986/87-95/96) plan for the sector,
building upon and extending the progress achieved in the first phase, and
strengthening and intensifying the reform process in selected areas.   The
project would provide financial assistance for specific investments
constituting a time slice of the public expenditure plan for the sector.
Funding for the various project components would become available as
successive steps of the reform process are achieved. There would be no
fast-disbursing element of funds against an import list or otherwise
unrelated to specific expenditures within the sector. Thus, the project
would be a sector investment credit, rooted in specified components, but
with a strong policy-based orientation and content.
4.03      The relationship of this project to the broader public
expenditure plan for the period 1990191-95/96 is summarized ir. Table 4.1.
The total investment required for this period under the revised plan
adopted by the Government in consultation with IDA is MK420.20 million.
This requirement would be met from the sources shown in the table.
Financing commitments for the period total 54.7 percent, and it is
anticipated that other donors (EC and possibly the Dutch Govt through WHO)
will provide an additional MK31.9 million.  To complete the program, an
additional MR158.21 million is required.  The Government would provide an
additional MK16.1 million out of national budgetary rosources, and to close
the gap an IDA Credit of MK142.1 million (US$55.5 million) is proposed.  In
addition, the Government has agreed to provide, from its own resources, an
increas, in the annual health recurrent budget, so that the health share of
the national budget would reach at least 9.1 percent by the end of the plan
period (Annex A).

4.04      The policy reform content of the project is summarized in the
Letter of Health Policy and associated policy matrix in Annex A. In order
to ensure effective and expeditious implementation of the reform package,
there will be an annual review of the reform program. These reviews will
have the following objectives: (a) to monitor the implementation of the
program; (b) to ensure adherence to the program objectives; and (c) to
review adherence to disbursement conditions and performance targets. The
annual review and mid-term review conditions are provided in Annex B. In
addition, there will be a mid-term review in 1993 to review program
implementation and to take stock of progress (para 4.39).
4.05      The activities and expenditures the project will support are in
line with the objectives stated in para 4.01, and can be briefly summarized



- 38 -
as follows (see next section for further details). The projects' component
on strengthening of basic programs will include: (a) for primary health
care and maternal and child health care, development and expansion of the
Health Surveillance Assistants into outreach-oriented and village-based PHN
promoters and service providers; rehabilitation and/or construction of
rural health centers; provision of rural housing where needed to attract
essential personnel; and provision of some vehicles to improve the
effectiveness of district level services; (b) for malaria, improvements in
logistical support, in the supply of anti-malarial drugs, and in support
for drug resistance monitoring; (c) for AIDS, acceleration of efforts in
education and prevention campaigns and in acquisition of blood screening
equipment; (d) for child spacing, launching of a National Family Welfare
Council of Malawi to promote family planning and expansion of the MOH's
program; (e) for women in development, institutional strengthening,
upgrading of training activities, and testing of small scale demonstration
projects on appropriate technology dissemination and credit provision; and
(f) for nutrition, extension of a community-level food security approach
developed and tested by UNICEF, and a micronutrient deficiency program.
The component on strengthening of support services will includes (a) for
staffing issues, upgrading of training programs and facilities for health
cadres currently in critically short supply, and enhancement of personnel
management information and planning capabilities; (b) for the IEC program,
expanded activities for training health workers, and provision of resources
for conveying population, health, and nutrition messages to communities;
and 5c) for pharmaceuticals, technical assistance and training on drug
procurement, distribution and production, and improvement of facilities and
equipment. The component on efficiency improvements will include (a)
rehabilitation of three urban health centers and construction of two others
to shift more outpatient care away from the more costly and overcrowded
hospitals, (b) rehabilitation of one district hospital, construction of
another, and replacement of worn-out equipment in selected other hospitals;
Cc) introduction of reforms in patient fees; and (d) budget reforms
involving increases in the MOH budget and the share of it devoted to
outreach and peripheral health services. The component on the social
dimensions of adiustment initiative will include support for "social
support program fund' to assist small scale pilot interventions derived
from felt needs of communities and expressed through Government or NGO
channels.
B. Detailed Description
Strengthening of Basic Programs
4.06      To support the improvement of PHC/MCH services at the periphery
(para 3.12), the project would provide assistance in three main areas.
First, it would support the salaries (i.e. incremental personnel
compensation costs, on a declining basis) and equipment costs (bicycles and
kits) of 3,550 additional HSAs. The training of the additional HSAs,
together with the training of supervisors drawn from the health center
teams, is to be supported by USAID. Second, the project would fund the
rehabilitation and/or construction and equipment of about 30 rural health
centers (para 3.11). Third, the project would finance improvements in
other critical aspects of infrastructure needed to support rural PHN/MCH



- 39 -
services. Specifically, because housing is a key constraint to service
expansion at the periphery (para 2.42), the project would fund the
construction of about 462 new rural houses and rehabilitation of about 50
existing ones (para 3.1). Also, because transport is another major
constraint, 24 four wheel drive vehicles would be provided for the district
services.
4.07      To support efforts on malaria and AIDS, the project would assist
collaborative efforts with other donors including especially the
substantial initiatives being aided by USAID. On malaria (paras 2.45,
3.15), the project would fund about 15 vehicles, with two at the national
level, three at the regional level, and ten at the district level. On AIDS
(paras 2.46, 3.16), the project would fund activities presently not fully
supported by other donors (para 2.73), including some laboratory equipment
and supplies, patient counselling and home-care, and the production and
distribution of educational materials for schools and colleges.
4.08      To support expansion and improvement of the CS program (paras
2.41, 3.17), the project would, with a similar goal of collaboratively
assisting initiatives being aided by USAID and others, provide funds for
the establishment of the Family Welfare Council through the const-uction,
furnishing and equipping of a modest office, provision of about three four-
wheel drive vehicles, and funds for the training of community-based workers
and preparation of IEC materials. The project would also fund the salaries
of 3 senior MOH staff at headquarters, and the construction, furnishing,
and equipping of 12 fixed facilities for CS in existing health centers.
4.09      To support enhancement of WID actions the project would
strengthen the MOCS by refocusing its work from the current "welfare
approach" to an integrated development approach (paras 2.50-2.51, 3.18).
Inputs would be provided for three main activities. First, the project
would strengthen the institutional capacity of the MOCS WID program through
technical assistance (two long-term advisors for a total of 72 person-
months, 36 person-months of short-term technical assistance and 18 person-
months of low-cost consultants for technical skill development); through
technical training (50 person-months of overseas technical training in 5
village industries and bio-gas construction and management, overseas study
tours for 7 officers, and regular in-service training and workshops) for
staff and for rural women and leaders; and also through support for the
administration of the program within the MOCS (including financing on a
declining basis of some of the incremental salary costs of about 3 new
staff, training activities and the purchase and running costs of 6
vehicles, 25 motorcycles and 55 bicycles). This part of the project
complements the proposed ODA assistance to MOCS to develop project
preparation capacity. Second, the project would support the improvement
and reorganization of Magomero Community Development College, through
upgrading of the physical infrastructure, provision of equipment and
working capital for five village industries to be taught, training
activities and courses, and curriculum revision. Third, the project would
support small demonstration projects in three districts, through
mobilization of women's groups and the provision of small amounts of credit
for income generating activities linked with other basic needs services.
As a condition of disbursement, the Government will present to IDA an



- 40 -
acceptable proposal for the administration of the provision of credit (para
6.04). The demonstration projects would emphasize the application of
appropriate, low-cost technologies, including bio-gas and maize mills.
4.10      To support nutrition and food security at the community level
(paras 3.19-3.20), the project would help finance the training and
equipping of 5,500 CHVs. Support also would be provided to expand and
carefully evaluate the following three experimental models of community-
based nutrition innovationst (a) provision of food supplements through the
WFP; (b) income generation activities and the introduction of labor-saving
technologies for women to reduce their caloric expenditure, this sub-
component is linked to the demonstration projects supported by the WID
component, (para 4.08); and (c) area-based, integrated programs including
packages of agricultural inputs and foo'k for lowest income women. Funds
would be provided for ongoing evaluaticns and review meetings. The project
would finance a nutrition module in the 1991 National Sample Survey of
Agriculture. With respect to micronutrionts, the project would complement
the ongoing support from UNICEF by financing the purchase of iodized oil
capsules, ferrous sulfate and folic acid tablets, materials and evaluation
activities. The project also would finance a study of the Malawi salt
distribution system to identify means by which the Government could reduce
salt prices, and to assess the feasibility of salt iodization.
Strenatheninx of Support Services
4.11      To increase the supply of manpower essential for the delivery of
PHC services and to strengthen the management of human resources (para
3.21), the project would expand training capacity for medical assistants,
health assistants, ENMs and registered nurses at the Lilongwe School of
Health Sciences, the Blantyre Medical Assistant School, the Zomba School of
Nursing and the Blantyre and Lilongwe campus of the Kamuzu College of
Nursing through the construction and rehabilitation of student hostels. In
addition, a new school for the training of ENMs would be built in Lilongwe,
along with additional student hostels at the district hospitals. The
supply of nurse tutors would be expanded through the training of 80 nurse
tutors at Kamuzu College of Nursing, and the ability to deploy these tutors
would be supported through the provision of staff housing at both MOH and
PHAM nursing schools.
4.12      To complement the ongoing efforts of the MOH to strengthen its
internal management, the project would provide assistance for the
decentralization of the personnel management functions to the regional and
district levels by providing technical assistance, equipment and training
to develop a personnel and management information system. In addition, to
support he district peripheral health service administration being set up
by the Government, the project would fund the training and incremental
salary costs of 24 district peripheral health administrators and 30
district accountants (para 3.24 - 3.25).
4.13      To improve the IEC activities and capabilities of the PHN
sectors, the project would finance: materials development for print, radio
and audiolvisual use; community IEC outreach activities; research and
evaluation activities; and short-term technical assistance (para 3.23).



- 41 -
The activities supported would ha-e a national focus: the critical target
audiences would be the rural needy and particularly women and children.
Furthermore, the assistance would complement and build on the activities
and resources provided by other donor support programs (in particular, the
USAID-funded PHICS project and the IDA-funded Second Family Health
Project).
4.14      To improve further the effectiveness and efficiency of the
pharmaceutical system (paras 2.58, 3.22), the project would support those
activities in the national pharmaceutical program not covered by the
proposal being submitted to the Netherlands Government. It would provide
funds for: (a) interregional training of pharmacists; (b) technical
assistance in stores management, central administration and training of
pharmaceutical personne'; (c) rehabilitation and adjustments to the
existing stores in Lilongwe and Blantyre; and (d) additional trucks for
interregional transport of supplies. WHO would be the technical agency
responsible for supervision of the implementation of external support to
the pharmaceutical program.
Efficiency Improvements
4.15      For the last several years, the MOH has been concerned to improve
the efficiency of its hospital services, particularly in relation to the
major hospitals located in Lilongwe (Kamuzu Central Hospital), Blantyre
(Queen Elizabeth Hospital) and Zomba (General Hospital). These hospitals
currently consume over a third of the revenue/recurrent budget of the MOH,
have regularly had budget. over-expenditures, and are believed to operate
extremely inefficiently. To improve their efficiency, it will be necessary
for the MOH to undertake a comprehensive program of investment and reform,
not only in the hospitals themselves, but also through improving the
quality of peripheral services and by encouraging patients to make greater
use of these peripheral services. The project supports actions within the
hospitals to reduce costs, and across the whole health care system to
reduce congestion in the major hospitals and encourage patients to seek
care at the lowest appropriate tier of the system.
4.16      Reducing Costs within the Major Hospitals.  MOH has reviewed the
main items of non-salary expenditures - vehicle operation and maintenance,
boiler and electrical systems, water, telephones, and food consumption - to
find ways to reduce these expenditures. (Expenditures on pharmaceutical
and medical supplies were co;isidered separately, and the proposed
pharmaceutical component of this project includes a number of measures to
improve prescribing practices.)
4.17      MOH has submitted to IDA five-year efficiency improvement plans
for the three major hospitals. Detailed implementation plans for 1991/92
have also been submitted, and will be submitted annually for later years
for IDA approval. The proposals for cost reductions tall into four broad
categories: (a) transport; (b) utility systems; (c) other expenditure
items; and (d) improved accounting and expenditure control capacity.
Specific proposed actions include: training drivers and equipment
operators in proper use and preventive maintenance and servicing of
vehicles and equipment; instituting systems for more efficient use and



- 42 -
control of transport and telephones; i& talling water meters and checking
and repairing leaks; replacement of old boilers and kitchen and laundry
equipment where breakdowns are frequent, and significant savings would be
realised on high repair and running costs; security improvements (lights,
fencing) to reduce theft; improvements to food storage and preparation
facilities to reduce wastage; on-the-job training in hospital financial
management; and selected studies of costs and savings of ideas for other
proposals for later years.
4.18      System-wide Efficiency-improving Measures.  Efficiency will also
be improved through investments at various levels of the health care system
that will reduce congestion at the three major hospitals and will encourage
patients to seek care at appropriate, lower tiers of the system. In order
to reduce congestion, the project will fund the construction of two new
urban health centers in Blantyre and Lilongwe, rehabilitation of six
existing ones in the three cities (para 3.27), construction of a new
district hosp4tal for Lilongwe District and the replacement of the existing
dilapidated hospital at Chiradzulu.
4.19      Efforts to strengthen preventive and community health care
actions and rural health centers will assist in reducing hospital
congestion and promoting efficient use of all levels of the health care
system. In addition to the activities already described, the project will
support MOH's efforts to explore ways to encourage stronger community PHC
activities through financing the establishment of a PHC fund (details are
set out in Annex D). Procedures for communities to make proposals to the
fund and clear guidelines for the sort of activities that will be eligible,
will be established before project effectiveness. The fund would (a)
provide low-cost support to communities to carry out PHC activities at the
local level; (b) provide resources to encourage innovative health financing
schemes within communities; (c) finance rewards of various kinds to
communities which are judged to have been particularly successful in PHC
activities; and (d) provide support to enable PHC activities to be
monitored by health workers. The scheme will be monitored carefully and
evaluated to determine whether and hor it should continue (para 3.27).
4.20      The project will also fund studies to (a) define options for
increasing cost sharing, and (b) to evaluate the impact of the cost sharing
measures as they are introduced, and (c) to evaluate progress under the
decentralisation program.
Social Program Support Fund
4.21      To support the Government's initiative on the social dimensions
of adjustment, the project would contribute to the financing of a "social
program support fund", which would assist small-scale pilot interventions
derived from the felt needs of communities and expressed through existing
Government or NGO channels. In all cases these would be based on full and
early consultation with the communities concerned, ir. order to encourage a
participatory approach. In most cases there would be some contributions
from the communities themselves through self-help, especially through their
own labor. The social program support fund would be administered by the
Social Support Task Force, chaired by DEPD and consisting of



- 43 -
representatives of the Ministry of Finance, Ministry of Education and
Culture, Ministry of Labor, MOA, MOLG, MOCS and MOH. This task force which
has been established with its Social Support Program Coordinator, would be
responsible for the identification and monitoring of the pilot
interventions and would transmit the experiences to the Special Studies
Unit of the DEPD. The pilot activities would enable the Government to
decide on instruments, programs, and policies which could be implemented
nationally to promote community and social welfare development. The
project would also support the carrying out of a number of studies ana
evaluations in areas of high priority (Annex E). The pilot activities
would be supported by this credit and funds from other donors. In
addition, for its efficient implementation, the project would fund ttree
staff years of technical assistance for a project manager, three person-
years for a procurement specialist, and three person-years for a local
architect, as well as related operating expenses.
C. Project Cost
4.22      Summary of Proiect Costs.  The total cost of the proposed
project, including Government's contribution, is estimated at about US$74.3
million equivalent, excluding customs duties and taxes equivalent to US$2.3
million. The estimated costs and local and foreign exchange shares are
given by component and category of expenditure in Tables 4.2 and 4.3 and
are further elaborated in Annex T13.

4.23            Basis of Cost Estimates.  Construction costs are based on reviews
of recently awarded contracts for similar facilities being built under on-
going IDA projects. Furniture costs for new facilities are based on
existing priced furniture lists and amount to 5 percent of the cost of new
construction. Drugs costs are at CIF value. Equipment Ai1d vehicle costs
have been calculated on the besis of existing equipmenc lists and recent
procurement under on-going projects. External terlinical assistance and
fellowship costs--US$100,000 per staff year of specialist assistance and
US$20,000 for external training--have been estimated on the basis of recent
prices for comparable technical assistance in Credit 1768-MAI. Local
consultants costs are based on the prevailing rates in the country.
Technical assistance costs include housing, travel, relocation costs,
subsistence, office services, fees and recruitment costs. Overheads are
separately costed. The cost of MK7,000 per student-year for training at
the local polytechnic is based on recurrent student costs. Local salaries



- 46 -
are based on civil service rates. Base cost estimates reflect prices as
projected for the time of negotiation.
4.24      Customs Duties and Taxes.  In line with Malawian policies and
practices, items imported especially for the proposed investment program
would be exempted from customs duties and taxes. Certain items are
expected to be purchased locally on the open market and reimbursement
percentages have been calculated to exclude duties and taxes estimated at
US$2.3 million equivalent on such items as imported building materials
under construction contracts, and local purchase of supplies and materials,
etc.
4.25      Contingency Allowances.  Physical contingencies of 10 percent
have been added to the base costs of civil works and 5 percent to the base
costs of furniture and equipment. Price contingencies were calculated
based on Bank staff estimates of international and domestic inflation; and
have been applied in :zcordance w'th the agreed timetable for activities to
begin; (Table T18) on the assumption of the following annual rates of
increase:
Table 4.4s Assumed Inflation Rates
1989    1990    1991    1992    1993    1994       1995
Foreign        -0.4      4.9     4.9      4.9     4.9     4.9       4.9
Local           15.8     9.0     5.0      5.0     5.0      5.0      4.0
Source:  Bank Staff (Country Operations) Calculations.
4.26      Foreign Exchange Component.  The foreign exchange component,
estimated at US$50 million equivalent or 65 percent of the total Project
investment cost net of taxes, has been calculated as follows: (a) civil
works, 65 percent; (b) professional services at 60 percent; (c) furniture
made by local craftsmen, 30 percent, and other furniture, 100 percent; (d)
equipment and vehicles, 100 percent; (f) drugs, 100 percent; (g) technical
assistance and overseas fellowships, 100 percent; and (h) local training
and workshops, 50 percent. The foreign exchange estimates take into
account that the bulk of the equipment and a considerable part of the
building materials would be imported.
4.27      Recurrent Costs.  Under the proposed project additional recurrent
costs would be generated by (a) recruitment of staff to fill the additional
posts in the Project implementation Unit, and salaries of additional staff
for the regional and district health offices, and (b) operation and
maintenance of new buildings, vehicles and equipment. The total additional
recurrent costs generated by these items would be US$6.3 million over the
project life. Consistent with the Bank's strategy for Malawi as discussed
in the Public Expenditure Review (Report 7281-MAI), the project would
finance a substantial portion of these costs (90 percent) on a delivery
basis. Local salaries would be financed on a sliding scale, with the
project paying all of the increment initially, and the Government absorbing
these gradually over five years (para 4.32(e)). At project completion, the
additional annual recurrent costs would amount to 15 percent of the 1990/91



- 47 -
MOH recurrent budget, however, 92.2 percent of this is for salaries of the
additional 3050 HSAs to be deployed. The long-term recurrent cost
implications of the project are minimized by (a) the very limited capacity
expansion through construction of new facilities and (b) the agreed
efficiency enhancing measures, which would reduce the cest of managing the
central hospitals in Blantyre, Lilongwe and Zomba.
D. Financing Plan
4.28      The IDA Project of US$55.50 million would finance 74.7 percent of
the total project cost net of taxes, or 81.9 percent of the foreign
exchange component and 62.1 percent of local costs net of taxes.
Government would finance 8.5 percent of total project costs or 10.2X of the
total cost exclusive of EC and WHO financing. EC has tentatively agreed to
parallel financing 231 new rural houses, rehabilitating 24 rural houses and
the construction of Chiradzulu District Hospital, amounting to US$11.1
million. The Dutch Government through WHO has been approached to parallel
finance part of the pharmaceutical components. The financing plan is
summarized in Table 4.5.

(a)  Civil works valued at US$21 million would be procured through
International Competitive Bidding (ICB) in accordance with Bank
Group guidelines. Civil works aggregated at $1.0 million would
be procured through Local Competitive Bidding (LCB) since these
would consist of a large number of small value contracts which
are scattered all over the country and are spread over time. The
average value of each contract is about US$150,000. LCB
procedures have been reviewed and are generally acceptable.
These LCB procedures will include loca1 advertising, public bid
opening, disclosure of evaluation criteria, and award to the
lowest evaluated bidder; the first set of dociunents will be
subject to IDA review.;
(b)  Goods required for the project include equipment, vehicles,
furniture, drugs;
(i) Equipment and vehicles, valued at US$10.3 million would be
procured through ICB in accordance with Bank Group
guidelines. Materials/supplies such as paper and seeds
required in small lots and quantities during the lifetime of
the project, valued at US$0.5 million would be most
appropriately procured through international or local
shopping with quotations from a minimum of three bidders.
The average value of each of these contracts is expected to
be lower than US$2,000;
(ii) Furniture valued at US$0.9 million would be procured through
LCB since these are simple items locally available. Drugs
and other materials valued at US$4.7 million would be
procured through ICB, in accordance with the Bank Group
guidelines; and
(iii) Funds. This activity is not subject to procurement and will
be utilized as per the details given in Annex D and E. IDA
will, however, monitor this activity annually as per details
in Annex B.
(c)  Services.  Technical assistance would be recruited under the Bank
Guidelines for the Use of Consultants, 1981. Professional
services for civil works would be selected in accordance with
Bank Group guidelines. All fellowships and training workshops
and studies financed by the project would be carried out under
terms and conditions acceptable to IDA 6/; and
(d)  Recurrent costs consisting of local salaries US$3.5 million and
operating costs US$3.7 million would follow regular Government
procedures and would be reimbursed on the basis of actual
expenditures. The operating costs include fuel, maintenance of
vehicles and equipment including data processing facilities.
6/Annex G for details of Technical Assistance, Fellowship and Studies.



- 50 -
4.30      The threshold for prior review by IDA would be US$100,000.  More
than 75 percent of procurement contracts would be subjected to prior
review. The first LCB documents for goods and works would be reviewed by
IDA before its release for bidding.
F. Disbursement
4.31      Disbursement from the Special Account would be against agreed
expenditures. Disbursement would be on the basis of:
(a) 100 percent of foreign expenditures and 80 percent of local
expenditures for civil works and professional services;
(b) 100 percent of foreign and local expenditures for furniture,
equipment, vehicles and transportation of furniture and
equipment to health centers and hospital sites;
(c) 100 percent of foreign expenditures and 80 percent of local
expenditures for drugs;
id) 100 percent of foreign expenditures and 80 percent of local
expenditures for technical assistance, fellowships and
training;
(e) 100 percent of salaries of local staff (excluding salaries
of staff of Project Unit) for the first two years. 65
percent for third and fourth year of project life and
thereafter at 30 percent; and 100 percent of the workshops;
(f) 80 percent of operational expenditures for project
administration; and
(g) 100 percent of funds including pilot program funds.
Simplified disbursement procedures on the basis of statements of
expenditures would be allowed for the following items: (a) civil works
contracts of less than US$100,000 equivalent and other contracts of less
than US$50,000 equivalent; and (b) in-service training costs and
operational expenditures of the Project Unit and the Planning Section of
the MOH. An indicative schedule of disbursement is shown In Annex T15.
4.32      Special Account.  A special account of the equivalent of the
amount US$2.0 million covering 6 months expenditures would be kept in a
commercial bank account on terms and conditions acceptable to IDA, to meet
eligible expenditures for goods and services. Replenishment applications
would be accompanied by monthly statements from the commercial bank,
suitably cross referenced by the PIU to the related applications. Amounts
to be replenished would take into account the limits for each part of the
project.



- 51 -
G. Implementation
4.33      The implementation of the reform program is to be carried out by
the Planning Section of MOH through the Committee on Monitoring and
Evaluation which will be set up under this Project. This Committee, which
is chaired by the Principal Secretary, MOH, would be responsible for
ensuring that the objectives of the program are met. The Committee is to
provide support to the Planning Section in the following areas: (a)
coordination among various sections of MOH, MOCS, MOLG and DEPD; (b)
preparation of the relevant reports for the annual reviews with the Bank
Group; and (c) monitoring and reporting on progress made in implementing
various policies. In addition, the Committee would supervise MOH's budget
requests (development and recurrent budgets) for consistency with
programmed activities, staff and policy action objectives. In order to
help ensure that the reform program is implemented smoothly and on
schedule, the Government is currently taking steps to strengthen the
planning function of the MOH so that it would be in a position to implement
the reform. In addition, the responsibility for the reform would be
directly with the Principal Secretary of MOH. In order to ensure that
funds are disbursed rapidly, the Project is supporting the recruitment of a
well qualified financial analyst to be based in the project implementation
unit to take care of forward planning of MOH as well as all procurement
issues. Physical implementation will be carried out by the project unit
established under the Second Family Health Project (Credit 1768-MAI). See
Annex T-16 for Physical Implementation and Annex A Attachment 1 for Policy
Implementation.
4.34      Project implementation is expected to take five years beginning
April 1991. The Project would be completed by December 31, 1996 and will
close by June 30, 1997.
4.35      Status of Prolect Preparation.  An identification mission took
place in October 1988 and a preparation mission in October 1989 and again
in February 1990 at which time the different elements of the reform package
were discussed with the Government. At each stage of the preparation
process, the Ministry of Health was actively involved. Additionally, the
Government undertook the review and reformulation of the policies embodied
in the reform.
4.36      Impact on the Environment.  The Project has no adverse impact on
the environment.
4.37      Impact on Women.  The main beneficiaries of this project will be
women (a) as the main users of health institutions; and (b) as the main
beneficiaries from the CS, nutrition and WID programs.
H. Reporting and Monitoring
4.38      Auditing, Monitoring and Reporting.  All project accounts,
including the special account, would be audited annually by an independent
auditor satisfactory to IDA. While MOH has as of September 30, 1990
submitted all due audited accounts, these accounts have not always been on

)pinion with respect to amounts withdrawn on the basis of statements of
axpenditure. With respect to this project, the audit statement would be
tubmitted nine months after the end of the fiscal year. Continuous
!onitoring of the progress of the reforms would be the responsibility of
:he strengthened planning section of MOH while day-to-day responsibility
Eor ensuring that available Project funds are disbursed in time would be
:he responsibility of the Project Unit. At each designated annual review

.roject implementation and achievements. The schedule and composition of
IDA supervision missions are presented in Annex F.

V. BENEFITS AND RISKS
A. Benefits
5.01      The proposed credit is designed to support the Government's
health program and key adjustment measures in the form of improved public
expenditure budgeting, increased expenditure on PHC, improved internal
efficiency and increased cost recovery. The credit would fund a major
strengthening of district health services; provide support for priority
programs to promote PHC, CS and WID activities, and to reduce the incidence
of malaria, AIDS and malnutrition; finance an accelerated program of
manpower development; and continue the institution building activities
initiated through the Second Family Health Project. Especially through
strengthening the management and efficiency of the health care system at
headquarters, district and peripheral levels, and by encouraging better
cooperation between the Government and other service providers
(particularly PHAM and the local authorities), the credit would improve the
rationalization and utilization of existing facilities, services and
resources.
5.02      While the Government's highly ambitious quantitative objectives
for the period, and especially the planned substantial reduction in infant
and child mortality, are not likely to be achieved by the mid-1990's, the
approach being supported with assistance from this credit should produce
significant results and all segments of the Malawian population would
benefit from the credit. In particular, the target of an increase in the
contraceptive prevalence to six percent by 1992 and to about 10 percent by
1995/96 activities should be attainable. The expansion of the CS program
would, in the long run, slow down the population growth rate, leading to
savings in public and private expenditures for social services--health,
education and social welfare. It would also mitigate the negative effects
of population on the environment due to excessive cultivation and over-
utilization of fuel wood.
5.03      Morbidity and mortality due to the six immunizable diseases
should be significantly reduced as a result of the project, and the
community-based program should substantially increase the use by mothers of
anti-malarial prophylaxis and oral rehydration therapy in treating
diarrhoeal diseases of their children. Children would benefit especially
from reduced morbidity and mortality as a result of both the health and the
nutrition interventions. Improved health status of children would increase
net enrollment ratios at primary schools, reduce drop-out rates and improve
their mental capacity to benefit from the teaching. Women,in particular,
would benefit from the project, through the expanded CS activities and from
the strengthening of the WID activities of the MOCS. Rural women would
also benefit from the proposed income generating activities to be funded by
the credit. The malaria and AIDS control programs would benefit the
entire population. Rural families would benefit from the expanded access
to peripheral facilities through the strengthened community-based and
outreach work, the expansion and upgrading of health infrastructure, and
the provision of logistical support for service providers. Furthermore,



- 54 -
the Government and other employers would benefit from increased
productivity by a healthier labor force.
5.04      The wide range of institution-building activities under the
project would also have major capacity-building benefits. Through a
combination of organizational changes, training of health care providers,
technical assistance and improvement of management systems, the MOH staff
would become more effective in the delivery of health services.
B. Risks
5.05      There are several potential risks involved in the successful
implementation of this project. First, Malawi's fiscal difficulties and
the constrained Government expenditures pose the risk that health
development and recurrent expenditures may not increase fast enough to
permit timely completion of the investment plan and adequate funding of the
expanded system. An associated risk is that the cost savings from
efficiency measures in the hospitals and from improved pharmaceutical
management may not be recycled into improving the quality of health
delivery, but might be diverted to other uses. The above risks are
minimized by the Government's commitment, stated in the Devpol, to allocate
more resources to health, and by the requirement that MOH submit its
forward budget estimates, after approval by the Ministry of Finance, for
IDA review.
5.06      Another risk concerns the introduction of cost sharing measures
and their effect on the demand for health services, especially in the rural
areas. Unless sensitively presented to the public, there might be strong
opposition to their introduction. This risk is minimized by the gradual
pace proposed, and that people are already being made aware of their
likelihood.
5.07    A final risk is that the staffing requirements for the
consolidation of peripheral services might prove difficult for Government
to meet. This risk is being minimized by the provision in the credit for
the training of district health administrators. Furthermore, the credit
provides for the system to be evaluated two years after its inception and
adjusted if necessary.



- 55 -
VI. AGREEMENTS REACHED AND RECOMMENDATIONS
6.01      Prior to nexotiation, the Government sent to the Bank:
(a) the Statement of Health Policy and the related Policy Action
Program (para 4.01 and Annex A) in line with the schedule agreed
with the Bank, the principal actions to be carried out include:
increase financial resources for the health sector,increase
budgetary and staff for the peripheral health services,
efficiency improvements in the central hospital system,
introduction of cost sharing, decentralization and
rationalization of district health services, strengthening of
manpower planning capacity;
(b) 5 year plan for each major hospital to improve efficiency and
reduce recurrent expenditures (paras 3.27, 4.15);
(c) a detailed plan for rationalization and management of the health
services of Local Authorities and MOH at the district level
(paras 2.63, 4.12 and Annex B);
(d) plan for the decentralization of management authority to the
district, including the establishment of the District Health
Management Teams and the District Health Planning and
Coordinating Committees (para 3.26 and Annex B);
(e) terms of reference and composition of the District Health
Management Teams and the District Health Planning and
Coordinating committees (Annex B);
(f) evidence that the proposed health Manpower Planning and
Development Unit will be established in the MOH (para 3.24);
(g) approved list from DPMT showing all the approved posts for each
of the individual health facilities at the district level (Local
Authority, PHAM and MOHS) (Annex B);
(h) a plan for deployment of all 91/92 graduates to individual health
facilities showing an increase in numbers of peripheral
facilities staff equal to the numbers of graduates (Annex B);
(i) evidence that Government has introduced a revised fee schedule
for private patients and has an implementation plan to cover full
cost of service by April 1992 and an assurance that the fees will
be reviewed and increased every year subsequently (Annex B); and
(j) plan for the introduction of cost sharing measures (paras 2.66,
3.27 and Annex B).

management health teams, and cost-sharing measures adopted by it.

6.01 (a));

Association.

forth in paras. 6.01 to 6.04 above, the project is suitable as a basis for
an IDA credit of US$55.5 million equivalent to the Republic of Malawi.

Dear Mr. Vice President,
LETTER OF HEALTH DEVELOPMENT POLICY
I am writing to you in connection with the proposed
Population, Health and Nutrition Sector Investment Credit,
and especially to inform you about policy decisions and
measures that the Government has already taken, or is about
to take, to improve the health status of Malawians.
2.      As  you are aware, the Ministry of Health has already
benefited from two projects financed by the International
Development Association (IDA), which, inter alia, helped to
strengthen   the  Ministry's   institutional   and   planning
capacity.    As  a result, a Ten-Year National Health Plan for
the  perind  1986-1995 was prepared,  and  the Government's
objectives for the health sector were later reconfirmed in
the Statement of Development Policy. The increased priority
to be given to the health sector was also a major feature of
the Government's prosentation at the 1990 Consultative Group
meeting  for Malawi.   These  documents  and other official
statements   emphasize  the Government's  commitment  to  the
health. sector, and our belief that improving the health
status of the people will make a major contribution to
Malawi's economic and social development. To achieve this
the Government is now planning to embark on a major and
comprehensive   development   of  the   sector, based  on  a
combination of policy changes and investments.



- 59 -
ANNEX A
Page 2 of 7
-2-
At the core of the programme will be an *nhanced development
effort   and   also   changes  in resource mobilization  and
utilization.   In particular, the Government will increase the
proportion   of the budget allocated to the health sector, and
will also take steps to use the available resources as
efficiently, effectively and equitably as possible. Another
key feature will be the rationalization of health services
especially at the district level.
ACHIEVEMENTS IN THE HEALTH SECTOR
3.       Despite  the  lack  of  adequate  financial and human
resources,    considerable  progress  has already been made in
addressing   the  country's    high mortality  and  morbidity
rates.    With  approximately 750 health facilities, about 80%
of the population now lives within 8 km of a service delivery
point.   The  available health services are heavily uitilized,
reflecting bzth the commitment of the health staff to serve
their patients and also the confidence of the communities in
them.    A programme has already been commenced to expand and
upgrade   the   infrastructure   further,  including  selected
district hospitals.   In  addition efforts are being made to
integrate    the   services   provided  by   non-governmental
organizations (especially the missions) and local authorities
with those of the Ministry of Health.
4.       Important   advances are  also being  made  in  the
development of primary health care, particularly through the
expanded programme on immunization which has achieved high
rates of vaccination of children. A start has alao been made
with the use of village-level volunteers to assist with
nutrition and other activities. And, finally, although the
child-spacing programme only started in the early 1980's the
number of mothers now spacing their children has increased
significantly.  At present, there are about 60,000 acceptors,
equivalent to about 3% of women in the reproductive age
group.   We  expect  this figure to rise significantly by the
mid 1990s.
PROPOSED REFORM 1991 - 1996
5.       The Government's  objective"  for the health sector
are  set  out  in  the Ten-Year  National   Health Plan.   In
particular, priority will be given to:
(a) increased coverage of peripheral and district
health services;
(b) manpower development;



-60-                   ANNEX A
Page 3 of 7
-3-
(c) management and support systems;
(d) family health care;
(e) disease prevention;and
(f) nutrition activities.
In accordance with this overall framework, and building on
activities   already   underway,   the   Government's  present
strategy encompasses three main features: providing increased
support   to   the   key   health  care  delivery  programmes;
strengthening service support systems; and improving and
upgrading management capacities.
6.       Although  the  objective of the present initiative is
to improve the health status of the people of Malawi, the
vast majority of whom live in rural areas, this requires
actions  at  all  levels  of  the  system:  central, regional,
district and local. In particular, progress in strengthening
the  key  programmes  and  support  system  will  require  an
enhanced management capacity. I am attaching to this letter
as  annexes  A  and  B    copies  of a Matrix showing the main
policy changes to be carried out by the Government. The
following paragraphs summarize the main features of this
policy action plan.
A. MANAGEMENT
(i) Financial Resources
7.      Adjustments will  be made to increase the proportion
of  the  revenue budget  allocated  to the Ministry of Health
from about 7.1% in Financial Year 1990/91 to at least 9.1% in
Financial  Year  1994/95.    The proportion of the Ministry of
Health budget allocated to peripheral services will also be
increased significantly to ensure that these services receive
more   priority   and  particularly  to cover  the  costs  of
peripheral and community-level health care workers. Although
the major hospitals' budgets are expected to increase in real
terms, as a proportion of the total Ministry of Health
budget, they will receive a steadily decreasing share as
peripheral  services  are  strengthened.      A  programme  to
improve the operational efficiency of the major hospitals
will be implemented, and additional steps will also be taken
to improve the accounting and expenditure control capacities
in these facilitie2



-61-                  ANNEX A
Page 4 of 7
-4-
S.       Where  donors  are supporting the recurrent budget of
the Ministry of Health, this support will in future be
accounted for under the revenue budget. On the development
side, the allocation to the Ministry will rise substantially
to at least K60 million in Financial Year 1991/92 and to NK70
million in Financial Year 1992/93 to accommodate the major
investments  planned.    The allocations will be necessary, in
particular,   for  the  expansion  of  the  manpower  training
infrastructure.    The Government will also try to ensure that
all health development activities presently supported by
donors will be included in the development budget, although
this may require a further increase in the development budget
of  the  Ministry  of  Health.     Annual meetings with donors
will    be  held  to  review  and  revise,  as  necessary, the
proposed budget for the forthcoming year.
9.       The  Government  is conscious of the need to increase
fees  for  private  patients.    A  revised  fee  schedule has
already been agreed, and was implemented in July, 1990. A
further revision will be introduced by April, 1992. To
improve   the   efficiency  of  service  utilization,  and  to
mobilize   additional   funds   for  the  health  sector,  the
Government plans to introduce some cost sharing measures
after a reasonable period of preparation. The preparatory
activities will include iL1jrovements in the infrastructure,
especially the upgrading of urban health centres which is
expected to result in the decongestion of the three Major
Hospitals in Blantyre, Zomba and Lilongwe.
(ii) Health Planning and System Rationalization
10.      The  Government  will rationalize the health services
at the district level in order to reduce some of the major
discrepancies  presently  existing.    All  peripheral  health
services at district level currently operated and managed by
the Ministry of Health and local authorities vwll be operated
and managed by District Health Management Teams. To ensure
that peripheral health services receiva additional resources
and improved management, the activities of District Health
Management Teams will focus on peripheral health services
with   sub-comittees   of  the   Teams   managing   district
hospitals.      District   Health  Planning  and  Coordinating
Committees will also be established in each district. Now
steps   will   also  be  taken  to  improve  coordination  and
collaboration between Ministry of Health and non-governmental
health services, especially mission health services.



- 62 -
ANNEX A
5                   Page 5 of 7
(iii) Staff
11.     With   the   planned   rapid  expansion  of  training
activities, improved management of staff also needs to be a
central feature of the GoverNment's strategy. Steps will,
therefore,   be   taken  to  establish  an  adequate  manpower
planning capacity within the Ministry of Health, and to
improve   coordination   between decisions  on  training  and
overall service development plans. The personnel management
function   within   the  Ministry  of  Health  will  also be
strengthened, and an efficient manpower information system
will  be  designed  and  set up.    Actions  will be taken to
improve the retention of health workers in the Civil Service,
both through improved management to reduce student drop-out
rates and through improved career prospects.
B. SERVICE SUPPORT
(i)  Peripheral Health Workers
12.     As  noted  earlier, Malawi has an extreme shortage of
health  personnel.    It  is  therefore  planned  to  increase
siqnificantly the training capacity, especially for cadres
essential      for the delivery  of primary  healthy  care.
Priority  will be given to staffing peripheral health services
rather  thais hospitals.   To expand community-based services,
the   namber   of  health  surveillance  assistants  will  be
increased considerably, up to a total of about 4,000 by
1995/96.     The  implementation  of  the  new,  comprehensive
strategy for the recruitment, training and deployment of
these workers will be monitored and evaluated carefully to
eisure that they have maximum impact on the communities
served.
(ii) Pharmaceuticals and Medical SupDlies
13.     While  many  improvements  have  already been made to
the pharmaceutical and medical supplies system, additional
steps will be taken to strengthen further this critical
support  system.   The  information  base will be augmented
through a survey of drug availability and usage at the
peripheral level, and management of stock levels and facility
requirements   will  then be  improved.    A  review  of the
financial management of the Central Medical Stores will also
be carried out, and a strategy will be designed to deal with
its existing debt problen and improve operational efficiency
and financial prospects in the future.



- 63 -
ANNEX A
Page 6 of 7
(iii) Buildings
14.      In   view    of   the   accelerated   infrastructure
development   programme  which   will  be  carried  out,  the
Government  plans   to  review the  appropriateness  of the
standard designs of several key health institutions. This
will be done for district hospitals, health centres and staff
houses, to ensure that they meet with current health care
delivery systems and improved procedures for the filtering
and referral of patients. The Government is also conscious
of the need to improve the maintenance of buildings and
medical  equipment,  and  a  strategy  (including    financial
requirements,   staffing   implications   and  demarcation  of
institutional responsibilities) will be prepared.
C. PROGRAMME SUPPORT
(i)  Information, Education and Commnication Proarame
15.     Although  health  education is a critical part of any
strategy to improve the health status of the people, the
impact of the ongoing health education programe has been
reduced through inadequate coordination,insufficient numbers
of trained personnel, and an inadequate core budget. A
comprehensive Information, Bducation and Communication (IEC)
plan will be prepared, including related activities of other
Ministries as well as the Ministry of Health. Particularly
important, will be the inclusion of messages about different
health care delivery programmes (such as malaria, child
spacing and AIDS) into the coordinated overall programme.
The    Health    Education    Unit    will  be   strengthened
institutionally with the formation of a steering committee
and  a programe planning  and  operations  committee.  The
training   curricula   of  key   workers   (such   as health
surveillance assistants) will include INC activities, and
training will be given to newly appointed health education
officrs.
(ii) Nutrition Programs
16.         Despite  the  Government'   ongoing  efforts,  the
problem of malnutrition remains serious. The Government has
reviewed its existing policies and has decided to develop
further its strategy for community-based nutrition and food
security.   This will  involve  institutional strengthening,
increased monitoring  and  evaluation activities, and further
study and analysis of the feasibility of targeting food
subsidies to the most vulnerable groups.. Government policy
towards dealing with iodine deficiency disease will also be
reviewed,  particularly  to  assess  the  viability  of salt
iodination.



- 64 -
ANNEX A
7                   Page 7 of 7
(iii) Child Spacina
17.        For  the  child  spacing programme, I would like to
highlight   the   Government's  intention  to  expand  service
delivery both within fixed facilities and also through a
pilot community-based programme. Efforts are also being made
to   ensure  full  integration with  other  health  education
activities.     However,  the  Government  recognizes  that  a
multi-sectoral approach to population issues is needed and a
population  coordinating  unit  has    been established in the
Office of the President and Cabinet to plan the integration
of  population factors into the development strategiess.     A
Family Welfare Council will be established outside Government
to  complement  our programmes.    This  Council  will assist
Government   in   its population  activity  coordination  and
advocacy work.
18.     Although    the    portfolio    responsibility    for
women-in-development activities falls under the Ministry of
Community Services, which has already prepared a project
component also proposed for IDA financing, I would like to
add that the implementation of this and associated policy
changes will also directly support our child spacing efforts.
(iv) AIDS Prouramme
19.     The main  policy change concerning the national AIDS
programme  will  be the  introduction  of a  strategy  for
home-based  health  care  for AIDS  patients.    In addition,
however, the Ministry of Health will ensure that AIDS-related
IEC activities are fully integrated into the comprehensive
IEC programme discussed earlier, and that education on AIDS
which has started in schools is strengthened.
Yours sincerely,
L. J  Ch ango
MINISTER OF FINANCE
