MALAWI                    ANNEX C
PHN SECTOR CREDIT               Page 1 of 5
Hospital Efficiency Improvement Component
1.   Backgrous.d.
For the last several years, the Ministry of Health (MOH) has been
concerned to improve the efficiency of its hospital services, particularly
in relation to the major hospitals located in Lilongwe (Kamuzu Central
Hospital), Blantyre (Queen Elizabeth Hospital) and Zomba (General
Hospital). These hospitals currently consume over a third of the
revenue/recurrent budget of the MOH, have regularly had budget over-
expenditures, and are believed to operate extremely inefficiently. To
improve their efficiency, it will be necessary for the MOH to undertake a
comprehensive program of investment and reform, not only in the hospitals
themselves, but also through improving the quality of peripheral services
and by encouraging patients to make greater use of these peripheral
services.
2.    Financial Context.
As described elsewhere, the project will provide considerable support
for the strengthening of peripheral services. In addition, the need to
undertake improvements within the hospitals themselves has become
increasingly urgent in view of the objective of the MOH to reorient its
revenue budget towards the periphery, and the consequent aim to reduce the
proportion of the budget allocated to the three major hospitals from 35
percent of the total in FY 1990/91 to 25 percent of the total in FY
1995/96. However, although there will be severe pressure on these
hospitals to reduce their operating costs due to the proportional decrease
in their budgetary allocation, their operating budgets would still be
increasing in real terms over the five year period of the project due to
the real growth of the overall budget, but at a much slower rate of growth
than the other, higher-priority services. Against this background, it will
be necessary for each of the hospitals to revise and update their plans for
efficiency improvements, with particular attention to ensuring their speedy
implementation.
3.    Detailed Description of Component.
A review has already been made by the MOH (and the administrative
staff of the three major hospital) of the main items of non-salary
expenditures - vehicle operation and maintenance, boiler and electrical
systems, water, telephones, and food consumption - and ways in which these
expenditures could be reduced. (Expenditureo on pharmaceutical and medical
supplies were considered separately, and the proposed pharmaceutical
component of this project includes a number of measures to improve
prescribing practices). The proposals for cost reductions fall into four
broad categories: (i) transport; (ii) utility systems; (iii) other
expenditure items; and (iv) improved accounting and expenditure control
capacity.



- 85 -
3.1   Transport                                                ANNEX C
Page 2 of 5
Background studies have been carried out in reviewing the transport
needs of the major hospitals. These studies have highlighted the need for
the replacement of old and uneconomic vehicles in poor working order, and
the need for suitable vehicles for bulk transport. The studies also
concluded that the improvement of the vehicle fleets would reduce, if not
eliminate, the need to hire vehicles at high cost for essential services.
Some additional vehicles have already been purchased and hospital services
have improved. However, so far there has only been a marginal reduction in
costs. There is a particular need to monitor, on a continuous basis,
expenditures on fuel, vehicle maintenance and repair, and vehicle hire.
The program to improve transport efficiency, therefore, needs to include
the following actions:
(i)   regular testing of vehicles to determine fuel consumption, and
introduction of random checks thereafter on fuel consumption
levels;
(ii) regular training of drivers to improve discipline and reduce
accidents;
(iii) introduction of a driver/mechanic grade to improve transport
management at major hospitals and, possibly, regional health
offices;
(iv) review of incentives for good performance by drivers;
(v)   acquisition of motor cycles for despatch work at major
institutions;
(vi) improved carrying out of supervision of transport maintenance;
(vii) review of transport fleet, elimination of uneconomic vehicles,
and cost-effective purchase of appropriate replacement
vehicles; and
(viii) improved stock of vehicle spares to reduce rate of vehicles
off-the-road.
3.2   Utilities.
(i)   Boiler and electrical systems
At QEH there are 'whree coal-fired boilers used for steam generation
at the hospital for the operation of equipment in the kitchen, laundry,
CSSD and other units. The boilers and the reticulation system are over 30
years old, inefficient and need replacement. An engineering report on the
system has been carried out and various options were put forward to the MOH
for correcting the problems. One option was costed at MK 1.3 million and
another at MK 1.9 million. Some particularly urgent work has already
started to replace the boiler feed pumps with ball valves and the steam
heating coils in the feed tanks. However, a decision on a detailed plan of
action for a more comprehensive program of efficiency improvement has
awaited a signal that funds could be made available. It is now necessary,
therefore, for a plan to be finalized to correct these major utility
problems at the QEH. Suclh a plan should include:
(a)   an assessment of laundry machinery and equipment, and
development of a scheme for improvement;



- 86 -
ANNEX C
Page 3 of 5
(b)   organization of a scheme for replacement of the existing coal-
fired boiler system with an electrical reticulation system and
appropriate equipment for the laundry, kitchen and CSSD;
(c)   arrangements for all housing units presently supplied with
electricity from the hospital system to be metered separately
with bills to be paid by the occupants; and
(d)   measures to ensure that units not belonging to the hospital are
made responsible for electricity payments.
At QEH, apart from the boiler and associated reticulation problem,
there has also been concern that the electri"al distribution system needs
to be upgraded.  A consultancy report has recommended that remedial work
estimated at MK 60,000 be carried out, and that additional improvements
(costing MK 1.8 million) should also be undertaken to upgrade the
switchgear and to provide standby diesel generators for the hospital.
At KCH, on the other hand, an engineering consultancy report on the
electrical services recommended that the existing diesel boiler system
should not be replaced with the installation of an electrical system.
However, some urgent measures were recommended to improve the efficiency of
the kitchen and laundry services, and this work is already underway. While
no major plan of action, therefore, needs to be developed for the boiler or
electrical system at KCH at present, the situation may need to be reviewed
further in due course.
At other major hospitals and institutions a review should be carried
out to analyze the cost of electricity and to identify possible savings.
Measures should also be taken to ensure that private consumers are billed
separately and directly.
(ii) Water consumption
There has been a long history to the problem of excessive water
consumption at QEH, but some improvements have recently been made through
corrective measures taken following a report by the Ministry of Works.
Nevertheleas, some further steps are still requireds
(a)   arrangement for all units, including private consumers and
other MOH units besides the hospital, to pay their water bills
separately and directly;
(b)   arrangement for the fire hydrant to be metered separately and
for water consumption to be paid by the City Council; and
(c)   monitoring of water consumption and any necessary
rationalization then to be carried out.
At all health institutions, measures need to be taken to conserve
water use and eliminate waste. This will require improved general
maintenance of hospital buildings and emphasis on the elimination of leaks
etc.



- 87 -
3.3   Other Expenditure Items.                         ANNEX C
Page 4 of 5
(i)   Telephone usa-e
Some action has already been taken to ensure that individuals pay for
their own private calls. This has been facilitated by the decentralized
control of institutional budgets. However, some further steps are needed
tot
(a)   arrange for payments to be made for private calls by
responsible officers;
(b)   ensure maintenance of proper records for all calls made on
official phones; and
(c)   arrange for periodic monitoring of phone expenditures and
payments.
(ii) Food
In 1984 a comparative study of food expenditures at the central
hospitals showed large discrepancies in unit amounts. Several measures
were suggested to improve efficiency and reduce c.osts, and since then
purchasing procedures for food items have improved. The number of
registered suppliers has been increased, and there is now stricter control
exercised over the receipt, usage and storage of food in the hospital
kitchens. There is now a need for a review of food expenditures over the
last two years at the major health institutions, and also a review of
procedures for estimating, procuring, receiving and utilizing food. The
feasibility of organizing hospital food supplies on a contract basis could
be studied too.
3.4   Accounting and Expenditure Control Capacity
Many of the proposed improvements in hospital efficiency will require
physical investment expenditures, but many improvements will depend more on
strengthened procedures and management. Key to this will be further
development of the MOH0a accounting and expenditure control capacity.
There is already a senior accountant posted to each of the central
hospitals, and each district hospital has an approved post for a senior
accountant (and 11 of them are already in post). In each central hospital
a computer has also recently been Installed. However, the accounting
capacity In the hospitals (and also in the regional offices) needs to be
upgraded further, and the MOH Chief Accountant has proposed that another 50
assistant accountant posts be established. Local training for these staff
could be provided, on a day release basis, through the Malawi College of
Accountancy, to provide them with the basic skills needs to carry out their
jobs offectively.
4.    Funding and Avoroval Process.
The cost of the infrastructure items proposed amount to about MK 3.2
million (approximately US 1.1 million), most of which is proposed to be
spent at Blantyre. These items have been reviewed and supported in



- 88 -
ANNEX C
Page 5 of 5
principle by a Committee on Efficiency Improvements, which has been
established in the MOH and which is chaired by the Ministry's Deputy
Secretary.
Under the health sector investment credit, it is proposed to include
a fund of MK 5 million to support measures to improve the efficiency of the
three major hospitals. This would be for infrastructure as well as other
types of investment. The procedures to be adopted would encompass two
steps. First, by the time of credit negotiations, each of the hospitals
would propose a 5-year plan (to be reviewed and approved by the MOH
Committee on Efficiency Improvements) for efficiency improvements through
cost reductions. (To the extent that the revenue generated from private
patient fees would be expected to be greater than any additional
expenditures incurred, then some modest rehabilitation of private patient
facilities could also be considered). The hospital plans would show the
investments needed to secure the zav4ngs, and particular attention should
be given to training activities and improved management and expenditure
control systems. The plans should link these investments to projections of
the overall hospital operating budgets, consistent with the MOH budgetary
framework. They should also explain how the efficiency improvement program
would be monitored to ensure that savings are achieved and that new areas
of undue expenditures do not arise. Second, after review at project
negotiations with IDA of the individual hospital 5-year plans, specific
annual work programs would be prepared. The first of these (i.e. for FY
1991/92) would be a condition of credit effectiveness. For any proposed
infrastructure investment costing over US$ 100,000, a separate technical
report would need to be prepared. Annual work programs for each of the
subsequent years of the project would be prepared by the January of each
year, and then submitted for the review and approval of IDA. With effect
from January 1992, the annual work programs should each include a progress
report on work already undertaken and an analysis of the cost savings
estimated to be accruing. Approval of work programs for FY 1992/93 - FY
1995/96 would be contingent upon evidence of satisfactory progress on
previous schemes.

Primary Health Care Fund
The MOH faces a major challenge to improve the health status of the
people of Malawi, but there is already evidence that services are well
utilized when made available. The average number of outpatient visits per
year is two, and children under five years of age each have almost four
contacts with the health system annually. The high rates of coverage of
the expanded program on immunization and of the antenatal care program also
indicate that access to some key services is already good. At the same
time, however, tignificant further progress will depend on two critical
strategies - increasing the number of peripheral health workers and
strengthening community participation in the primary health care system.
The former will be supported through the manpower development strategy of
the health sector credit, while complementary measures will be used to
promote increased community involvement in health. One of these measures
will be the establishment of a primary health care fund.
In 1986 the use of paid community health care workers was
discontinued, because that approach had not engendered sufficient community
participation or sense of responsibility by individuals for their own
health. In addition, the paid community health workers had been overloaded
with too many tasks. At that time then the Government decided instead to
rely on village level volunteers for major activities such as nucrition and
family planning. More recently the Government has decided also to
introduce the cadre of health surveillance assistants, who will assist
health assistants in promoting the provision of safe water supplies, In
establishing village health committees, and generally in Implementing child
survival activities. They will also work closely with the other frontline
health workers, such as the enrolled community health nurses, the enrolled
nurses/midwives and the medical assistants. All of these cadres, however,
need to be strengthened and provided with support to facilitate their
motivational work in the local communities.
It is in this context that the MOH has decided to explore ways of
encouraging stronger community PHC activities. To do this, a PHC fund
would be established and financed through the health sector credit. The
fund would: (i) provide low-cost support to communities to carry out PHC
activities at the local level; (ii) provide resources to encourage
innovative health financing schemes within communities; (iii) finance
rewards of various kinds to communities which are judged to have been
particularly successful in PHC activities; and (iv) provide support for PHC
activities to be monitored and accessed more efficiently by health workers.
The specific criteria for each of these categories are still being
developed and reviewed by the MOH. However, a budget of about USS 4,000
per district per year (or about US$ 0.5 million in total) would be made
available through the project. In order to administer the scheme, each
district would constitute a committee to evaluate proposals for financing



- 90 -
ANNEX D
Page 2 of 2
from the PHC fund, and each committee's recommendations would be sent to
the regional level for final approval. After the first year of operation
of the scheme, a progress report on ongoing activities would need to
accompany any recommendations sent to the regional level for further
funding approvals. An annual report on implementation of projects would be
compiled by each region for submission to the MOH.

Social Program Suoport Fund
The Social Program Support Fund consists of two principal components:
studies (US$ 300,000) and pilot interventions (US$ 2.0 million). This
annex provides a guide to implemencation.
The studies are intended to provide a basis for improving policies and
interventions to address poverty. The Special Studies Unit of EP&D will
coordinate the studies. The Task Force will agree on those selected for
funding. They will be carried out by government and other institutes and
agencies. Studies could be inter-disciplinary and should involve, when
possible, a range of methodologies: sociological, anthropological, etc.
Priority areas already identified by EP&D and the CSR include: food
security and nutrition of vulnerable groups; women in development (access
to credit, fertilizer and income generating activities); land constrained
households; and welfare in the estate sector. Other areas might include:
access to literacy, primary health care, population, rural water supplies.
The major objective cf the Social Support Program (SSP) is to enable
the Government of Malawi to fund pilot interventions that aim at improving
the situation of vulnerable groups within the framework the Government's
overall strategy for poverty alleviation and growth. Successful and cost-
effective pilot interventions can be considered for wide replication. They
will help to encourage and empower vulnerable groups including the poor
actively to participate in social and economic development. The project
will finance pilot projects and the operating costs of coordination. The
IDA funds are supplemented by grant funds from other Donors; an SDA project
document contains details.
A. Institutional Arrangements
A Social Support Task Force (SSTF) has been set up, chaired by EP&D
and including also the Ministries of Finance, Agriculture, Community
Services, Local Government, Labor, Health, and Education, the NSO, and the
CSR. The Council for Social Welfare Services has observer status.
The SSTP will be responsible for providing guidance and oversight to the
SDA activities, including (a) deciding on priorities tor studies; (b)
defining the target groups that will be the primary beneficiaries of the
pilot interventions; (c) cetting procedures and criteria for the selection,
monitoring, and evaluation of pilot interventions; (d) making
recommendations to Government about implementation on a wider scale of
pilot projects that are tested and found to be effective, workable and cost
effective.
The SSP will be coordinated by the Special Studies Unit of the EP&D.
A coordinator will be hired to assist the Head of the Unit. Resources
(office equipment, a vehicle, training as necessary) will be provided to
the Special Studies Unit and the Evaluation & Monitoring Unit of the EP&D



- 92 -
ANNEX E
Page 2 of 7
to enhance their capacity to implement the SDA project component. The
responsibilities of Head of the Special Studies Unit are as follows
Social Program Support Fund
(a) Managing the SPSF in accordance with GOM/IBRD financial management
procedures;
(b) Ensuring that accounting information is up to date.
Studies
(a) Overseeing the implementation of the Social policy studies carried out
by the Spocial Studies Unit.
(b) Subcontracting social policy studies to the Center for Social Research
and other relevant organizations or individuals.
(c) Evaluating the final reports with the assistance of the Special
Studies Unit and the CSR and making appropriate recommendations to the
SSTP and the UTF concerning their timely utilization for the purpose
of social policy formulation within macroeconomic framework.
(d) Liaising with the National Research Council in the process of social
policy studies approval.
Pilot Prolects
(a) Appraising project proposals in accordance with the agreed criteria
with the assistance of the Special Studies Unit and the CSR.
(b) Presenting the proposals with comments and recommndations to the SSTF
for review and final approval.
(c) Carrying out a final evaluation of the project with the assistance of
the CSR and make recommendations to the SSTF concerning future
widespread replicability.
(d) Maintaining contact and regular flow of information between the
Special Studies Unit, line Ministries and the NGOs.
Workshops
Overseeing the organization of local training workshops. The aim of
these workshops will be to clarify and discuss the SDA project
objectives and to assist implementing agencies in putting appropriate
monitoring and evaluation systems in place. The workshops will be
organized by the CSR and will involve all potential implementing
agencies.
B. Target Groups
The following groups have been identified as being in need of
immediate assistance, and are the target of the SDA activitiest
(a) households with malnourished children;
(b) land constrained households (those with less than .5 hectares) who are
likely to deplete their food stocks before the next harvest;
(c) female-headed households with less than .5 hectare, with particular
attention to those who do not receive significant remittances or



- 93 -
ANNEX E
Page 3 of 7
transfers. These households are likely to have little or no formal
education, little contact with agricultural extension, may be labor
short and own few items of farm equipment and make little use of
fertilizer;
(d) families in the estate sector, particularly those with only seasonal
or irregular work with very low incomes;
(e) very low income urban families.
C. Criteria for Pilot Schemes Selection
Funding priority will be given to pilot projects that meet the
criteria set out below, especially those with employment and income-
generating potential. There are already a number of ideas being considered
by GOM and othera in Malawi that would be consistent with these guidelines.
These include: targeted food subsidi2s, food for work schemes, credit
schemes targeted to the poor, pilot nutrition interventions, literacy, and
provision of clean water. These broad guidelines are intended to help in
the selection of pilot schemes, but should not be seen as a check list to
be rigidly adhered to.
(a) The pilots should be of direct benefit to the vulnerable groups
identified above.
(b) They should be small-scale pilot interventions which should derive
from the felt needs of the community (expressed through existing
government channels or NGOs). In all cases there should be evidence
of full and early consultation with the communities concerned in order
to encourage a participatory approach. It is expected that in most
cases there will be some contributions from the community through
self-help.
(c) As the pilot scheme fund is small (2 million dollars) project costs
should be no larger than MK200,OOO. No minimum project cost is
proposed, even very modest proposals would be considered.
(d) Proposals should have the potential for being implemented on a larger
scale if they prove successful, which makes it imports:.t that they be
affordable and cost-effective.
(e) The life of the project should not exceed th:ee years. This criteria
should be modified and adjusted as experience is gained through the
implementation of pilot interventions.
(f)  They should be consistent with Government's sector strategies and
investment programs.



- 94 -
ANNEX E
Page 4 of 7
(g) Projects should have low managerial complexity and executing agencies
should possess t.ae management and staffing capability needed to carry
out proposed interventions.
(h) Projects should not involve large start-up costs or heavy capital
expenditure.
(i) Proposals that would encourage off farm income-generating activities
especially for women would need to ensure adequate returns for time
and money invested i.e. they should not be resource intensive or
require large capital inputs.
D. Guidelines and Information Needed in Project Proposals
Oblectives should be stated, clearly setting out (i) the reasons for
undertaking the project; (ii) who is likely to benefit; and (iii) the
outcome hoped for and the specific results aimed at. The activities to be
undertaken should be clearly described. The proposal should explain how
the project will be monitored by the implementing agency. The duration of
the pilot project should be stated. Viability: the proposal should give
reasons for believing that the project will be sustainable and replicable.
It should al.so include consideration of factors which might affect the
success of the project:
- naturallenvironmental conditions;
- dependence upon non-project personnel in implementing the project;
- factors such as transportation, variable prices, storage capacity, market
demand (where relevant)
- the receptivity of the community to new ideas and methods and proposed
measures to take this into account;
- cultural and social factors which might affect project implementation;
- attention to the needs of women (timing, location, demands on labor and
time);
- availability of required skills for carrying out the project activiites.
Details of the implementing agency should be given, stating (i) the
agency's previous experience in executing similar projects; and (ii) the
agency's capability to start, execute and complete a project on time and
within an established budget.
Prolect Cost (Budget) A detailed breakdown should be given of the
amount of money requested on a year by year basis, showing all items to be
funded. The cost breakdown should include salaries, any equipment, office
space, furniture, transport needs (including fuel).



- 95 -
ANNEY E
Page 5 of 7
E. Pilot Proiect Monitoring
Monitoring and evaluation of the effectiveness and impact of the
pilots is an extremely important part of the project. It will enable
decisions and planning for replication of successful approaches. EP&D,
specifically the coordinator, will ensure that adequate monitoring and
evaluation is carried out. Periodic reports on each pilot should be
prepared, and the coordinator should visit each p oject at least each
quarter. The CSR would be hired to prepare final project impact
evaluations.
The Special Studies Unit, assisted by the CSR and the Council for
Social Welfare Services, will also be responsible for helping implementing
agents (i.e. NGOs and line Ministries) to carry out simple monitoring
procedures to i) collect baseline data (a simple profile of the community
could suffice for small projects), ii) apply simple measures of project
achievements, e.g. increased income, improved autrition, attendance at
health centers, increased community self-confidence in taking decisions.
F. Procedures for the Pilots Funds
Review of Applications to the Pilots Fund
GOM agencies, communities, NGOs, and District Development Committees
may submit proposals at any time to the coordinator. Incomplete proposals
will be returned to the submitting agency with comments and suggestions as
well as a request that the agency make improvements and resubmit the
proposal. By the end of each month, the coordinator will forward all
proposals with comments and recommendations to the SSTF for review and
approval at a meeting to be held within the first 2 weeks of the month.
Following its review, the SSTF will return the proposals to the
coordinator, indicating which have been approved and which have not, making
additional comments when necessary. By the 28th of the month, the Unit
will notify concerned the agencies or group which has submitted the
proposal whether or not it was approved for funding.
Contracting Procedures
Within two weeks of a proposal being approved, a contract will be
drawn up in duplicate, to be signed by the coordinator and the
NGO/PVOIDDC/GOM agency. The signatory NGO will be solely responsible to
the coordinator for proper implementation of the project covered by the
contract.
The signatory GOM agency/DDCIPVO/NGO will be responsible for procuring
all the human and material resources necessary for the implementation of
the project covered by the contract. All physical assets procured through



- 96 -
ANNEX E
Page t of 7
the project will become the property of the project's beneficiaries or the
implementing agency once the project is completed.
Under no circumstances will the funding level agreed upon be increased
once a contract has been signed.
Contract termination: The coordinator has the right to terminate a
contract if the implementing agency does not comply with the terms of the
contract. In these cases, the coordinator may demand partial or total
repayment of funds already disbursed. Should the GOM Agency/NGO/PVO/DDC
cancel the contract, the coordinator may demand partial or total repayment
of funds already disbursed. Repayment of funds must be made within one
month of contract cancellation.
Disbursement Procedures
Funding for approved pilot projects shall be paid in both foreign
exchange and Maluwi Kwacha in one or more installments, as specified in the
contract. The method of disbursement shall be periodic advances.
The first disbursement shall not be roleased until the NGO makes a
request to the coordinator indicating the date on which project
implementatior. is to begin. At the saLte time, the NGO must be able to
demonstrate that adequate financial/accounting procedures are in place (see
section C below). The first disbursement of funds shall cover a period of
three months. Subsequent disbursements will be made at the request of the
Implementing agency. Requests should be supported by a written progress
report which also documents (with invoices, receipts etc) how the previous
disbursement of funds have been spent, and indicates how the next
disbursement will be used.
Unless agreed otherwise in the contract, the initial request for funds
must normally be made within one month of project approval, with pr'ject
implementation beginning in the following month. Should this condition not
be met, the coordinator has the unilateral right to terminate the contract.
Financial and Accounting Procedures
Project implementing agencies must keep a separate account for each
contract. This includes a separate bank account and/or account book which
lists income and expenditures by date and conforms with generally accepted
accounting procedures. When appropriate, both local and foreign currency
accounts must be maintained.
The accounts book and related documentation must be made available to
IDA for review upon request. The book and documentation must be retained
by the agency for a period of three years following project completion.



- 97 -
ANNEX E
Page 7 of 7
Supporting documentation shall includes
-    original bank statements
-    invoices/receipts for purchases
-    accounting documents
-    statements of expenses
ReDorting Reauirements
The Implementing agency must notify the coordinator Lmmediately should
problems arise which might significantly delay project implementation as
described in the contract. The agency shall notify the coordinator in
writing of any significant modification in the nature of tho project
activities which necessitace significant budget line changes, and request
coordinator's approval of the modifications.
Interim reports on the progress of the project activities shall be
submitted with each request for disbursement of funds. Theoe reports shall
include a section describing how the project progress is proceeding and a
financial section indicating (i) how previous disbursements have been used
and (ii) how the next disbursement of funds will be spent.
Within 2 months of project completion, the Implementing agency should
submit a final report to the coordinator which gives a full description of
project activities and accomplishments; a description of problems
encountered and how they were resolved; and an analysis, if possible, of
the impact of the project on the target group. This final report must
include a detailed financial statement.
